<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Linux kernel</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>

<p>The <b>Linux kernel</b> is a monolithic <a href="/wiki/Unix-like" title="Unix-like">Unix-like</a> computer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operating_system_kernel" title="Operating system kernel">operating system kernel</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a> operating system is based on this kernel and deployed on both traditional computer systems such as <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">personal computers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Server_(computing)" title="Server (computing)">servers</a>, usually in the form of <a href="/wiki/Linux_distribution" title="Linux distribution">Linux distributions</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> and on various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embedded_device" title="Embedded device">embedded devices</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Router_(computing)" title="Router (computing)">routers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wireless_access_point" title="Wireless access point">wireless access points</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Private_branch_exchange" title="Private branch exchange">PBXes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Set-top_box" title="Set-top box">set-top boxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/FTA_receiver" title="FTA receiver">FTA receivers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smart_TV" title="Smart TV">smart TVs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Personal_video_recorder" title="Personal video recorder">PVRs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NAS_appliance" title="NAS appliance">NAS appliances</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a> operating system for <a href="/wiki/Tablet_computer" title="Tablet computer">tablet computers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Smartwatch" title="Smartwatch">smartwatches</a> is also based atop the Linux kernel. While the adoption on <a href="/wiki/Desktop_computer" title="Desktop computer">desktop computers</a> is low, Linux-based operating systems dominate nearly every other segment of computing, from mobile devices to <a href="/wiki/Mainframe_computer" title="Mainframe computer">mainframes</a>. As of November 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, all but two of the <a href="/wiki/TOP500" title="TOP500">world's 500 most powerful supercomputers</a> run Linux. The other two run AIX on IBM power 7 hardware.</p>
<p>The Linux kernel was conceived and created in 1991 by <a href="/wiki/Linus_Torvalds" title="Linus Torvalds">Linus Torvalds</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> for his personal computer and with no cross-platform intentions, but has since expanded to support a huge array of computer architectures, many more than other operating systems or kernels. Linux rapidly attracted developers and users who adopted it as the kernel for other <a href="/wiki/Free_software" title="Free software">free software</a> projects, notably the GNU Operating System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The Linux kernel has received contributions from nearly 12,000 programmers from more than 1,200 companies, including some of the largest software and hardware vendors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linux_kernel_API" title="Linux kernel API">Linux kernel API</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Application_programming_interface" title="Application programming interface">application programming interface</a> (API) through which user programs interact with the kernel, is meant to be very stable and to not break <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Userspace" title="Userspace">userspace</a> programs (some programs, such as those with GUIs, rely on other APIs as well). As part of the kernel's functionality, <a href="/wiki/Device_driver" title="Device driver">device drivers</a> control the hardware; "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linux_kernel_mainline" title="Linux kernel mainline">mainlined</a>" device drivers are also meant to be very stable. However, the interface between the kernel and <a href="/wiki/Loadable_kernel_module" title="Loadable kernel module">loadable kernel modules</a> (LKMs), unlike in many other kernels and operating systems, is not meant to be very stable by design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Linux kernel, developed by contributors worldwide, is a prominent example of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_and_open_source_software" title="Free and open source software">free and open source software</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Day-to-day development discussions take place on the <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel_mailing_list" title="Linux kernel mailing list">Linux kernel mailing list</a> (LKML). The Linux kernel is released under the <a href="/wiki/GNU_General_Public_License" title="GNU General Public License">GNU General Public License</a> version 2 (GPLv2),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COPYING_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-COPYING-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-torvalds2000-gplv2only_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-torvalds2000-gplv2only-16">[16]</a></sup> with some <a href="/wiki/Firmware" title="Firmware">firmware images</a> released under various non-free licenses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-firmware_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-firmware-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LinuxCon_Europe_Linus_Torvalds_03_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1357" data-file-width="975" height="306" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/LinuxCon_Europe_Linus_Torvalds_03_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-LinuxCon_Europe_Linus_Torvalds_03_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/LinuxCon_Europe_Linus_Torvalds_03_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-LinuxCon_Europe_Linus_Torvalds_03_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/LinuxCon_Europe_Linus_Torvalds_03_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-LinuxCon_Europe_Linus_Torvalds_03_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LinuxCon_Europe_Linus_Torvalds_03_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Linus Torvalds</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Linux" title="History of Linux">History of Linux</a></div>
<p>In April 1991, <a href="/wiki/Linus_Torvalds" title="Linus Torvalds">Linus Torvalds</a>, at the time a 21-year-old <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a> student at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Helsinki" title="University of Helsinki">University of Helsinki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, started working on some simple ideas for an operating system. He started with a <a href="/wiki/Context_switch" title="Context switch">task switcher</a> in <a href="/wiki/X86_assembly_language" title="X86 assembly language">Intel 80386 assembly language</a> and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudo_terminal" title="Pseudo terminal">terminal driver</a>. On 25 August 1991, Torvalds posted the following to <i>comp.os.minix</i>, a <a href="/wiki/Usenet_newsgroup" title="Usenet newsgroup">newsgroup</a> on <a href="/wiki/Usenet" title="Usenet">Usenet</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Torvlads25Aug91_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Torvlads25Aug91-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I'm doing a (free) operating system (just a hobby, won't be big and professional like gnu) for 386(486) <a href="/wiki/IBM_Personal_Computer/AT" title="IBM Personal Computer/AT">AT</a> clones. This has been brewing since April, and is starting to get ready. I'd like any feedback on things people like/dislike in minix, as my OS resembles it somewhat (same physical layout of the file-system (due to practical reasons) among other things).</p>
<p>I've currently ported <a href="/wiki/Bash_(Unix_shell)" title="Bash (Unix shell)">bash</a>(1.08) and <a href="/wiki/GNU_Compiler_Collection" title="GNU Compiler Collection">gcc</a>(1.40), and things seem to work. This implies that I'll get something practical within a few months [...] Yes - it's free of any minix code, and it has a multi-threaded fs. It is NOT portable (uses 386 task switching etc), and it probably never will support anything other than AT-harddisks, as that's all I have :-(.</p>
<p>[...] It's mostly in C, but most people wouldn't call what I write C. It uses every conceivable feature of the 386 I could find, as it was also a project to teach me about the 386. As already mentioned, it uses a <a href="/wiki/Memory_management_unit" title="Memory management unit">MMU</a>, for both paging (not to disk yet) and segmentation. It's the segmentation that makes it REALLY 386 dependent (every task has a 64Mb segment for code &amp; data - max 64 tasks in 4Gb. Anybody who needs more than 64Mb/task - tough cookies). [...] Some of my "C"-files (specifically mm.c) are almost as much assembler as C. [...] Unlike minix, I also happen to LIKE <a href="/wiki/Interrupt" title="Interrupt">interrupts</a>, so interrupts are handled without trying to hide the reason behind them.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>After that, many people contributed code to the project. Early on, the <a href="/wiki/MINIX" title="MINIX">MINIX</a> community contributed code and ideas to the Linux kernel. At the time, the <a href="/wiki/GNU_Project" title="GNU Project">GNU Project</a> had created many of the components required for a free operating system, but its own kernel, <a href="/wiki/GNU_Hurd" title="GNU Hurd">GNU Hurd</a>, was incomplete and unavailable. The <a href="/wiki/Berkeley_Software_Distribution" title="Berkeley Software Distribution">BSD</a> operating system had not yet freed itself from <a href="/wiki/Berkeley_Software_Distribution#Net.2F2_and_legal_troubles" title="Berkeley Software Distribution">legal encumbrances</a>. Despite the limited functionality of the early versions, Linux rapidly accumulated developers and users.</p>
<p>By September 1991, version 0.01 of the Linux kernel was released on the FTP server (ftp.funet.fi) of the Finnish University and Research Network (<a href="/wiki/FUNET" title="FUNET">FUNET</a>). It had 10,239 lines of code. In October 1991, version 0.02 of the Linux kernel was released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 1991, Linux kernel 0.11 was released. This version was the first to be <a href="/wiki/Self-hosting" title="Self-hosting">self-hosted</a> as Linux kernel 0.11 could be compiled by a computer running the same kernel version. When Torvalds released version 0.12 in February 1992, he adopted the <a href="/wiki/GNU_General_Public_License" title="GNU General Public License">GNU General Public License</a> (GPL) over his previous self-drafted license, which had not permitted commercial redistribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Relnotes-0.12_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Relnotes-0.12-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>A newsgroup known as <i>alt.os.linux</i> was started, and on 19 January 1992, the first post to alt.os.linux was made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> On 31 March 1992, alt.os.linux became <i>comp.os.linux</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/X_Window_System" title="X Window System">X Window System</a> was soon ported to Linux. In March 1992, Linux version 0.95 was the first to be capable of running X. This large version number jump (from 0.1x to 0.9x) was due to a feeling that a version 1.0 with no major missing pieces was imminent. However, this proved to be somewhat overoptimistic, and from 1993 to early 1994, 15 development versions of version 0.99 appeared.</p>
<p>On 14 March 1994, Linux kernel 1.0.0 was released, with 176,250 lines of code. In March 1995, Linux kernel 1.2.0 was released, with 310,950 lines of code.</p>
<p>Version 2 of the Linux kernel, released on 9 June 1996, was followed by additional major versions under the version 2 header:</p>
<ul>
<li>25 January 1999 –  release of Linux kernel 2.2.0 (1,800,847 lines of code)</li>
<li>18 December 1999 – <a href="/wiki/IBM_mainframe" title="IBM mainframe">IBM mainframe</a> patches for 2.2.13 were published, allowing Linux kernel to be used on enterprise-class machines</li>
<li>4 January 2001  –  release of Linux kernel 2.4.0 (3,377,902 lines of code)</li>
<li>17 December 2003  –  release of Linux kernel 2.6.0 (5,929,913 lines of code)</li>
</ul>
<p>Starting in 2004, the release process changed and new kernels started coming out on a regular schedule every 2–3 months, numbered 2.6.0, 2.6.1, up through 2.6.39.</p>
<p>On 21 July 2011, Torvalds announced the release of Linux kernel 3.0: "Gone are the 2.6.&lt;bignum&gt; days".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3.0_release_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-3.0_release-22">[22]</a></sup> The version bump is not about major technological changes when compared to Linux 2.6.39;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> it marks the kernel's 20th anniversary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The time-based release process remained the same.</p>
<p>Version 3.10 of the Linux kernel, released in June 2013, contains 15,803,499 lines of code,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> while the version 4.1, released in June 2015, has grown to over 19.5 million lines of code contributed by almost 14,000 programmers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tanenbaum.E2.80.93Torvalds_debate">Tanenbaum–Torvalds debate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Tanenbaum–Torvalds debate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tanenbaum%E2%80%93Torvalds_debate" title="Tanenbaum–Torvalds debate">Tanenbaum–Torvalds debate</a></div>
<p>The fact that Linux is a <a href="/wiki/Monolithic_kernel" title="Monolithic kernel">monolithic kernel</a> rather than a <a href="/wiki/Microkernel" title="Microkernel">microkernel</a> was the topic of a debate between <a href="/wiki/Andrew_S._Tanenbaum" title="Andrew S. Tanenbaum">Andrew S. Tanenbaum</a>, the creator of <a href="/wiki/MINIX" title="MINIX">MINIX</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Linus_Torvalds" title="Linus Torvalds">Linus Torvalds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The debate, started in 1992 on the <a href="/wiki/Usenet" title="Usenet">Usenet</a> discussion group <tt>comp.os.minix</tt>, was about Linux and kernel architecture in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Tanenbaum argued that microkernels are superior to monolithic kernels and that therefore Linux is obsolete. Unlike traditional monolithic kernels, device drivers in Linux are easily configured as <a href="/wiki/Loadable_kernel_module" title="Loadable kernel module">loadable kernel modules</a> and are loaded or unloaded while running the system. This subject was revisited on 9 May 2006,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> and on 12 May 2006 Tanenbaum wrote a position statement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popularity">Popularity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Popularity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The huge rise in popularity of the Android operating system, which includes the Linux kernel, has made the kernel the most popular choice for mobile devices, rivaling the <a href="/wiki/Installed_base" title="Installed base">installed base</a> of all other operating systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Including previous years, three billion Android smartphones were estimated to have been sold by the end of 2014.</p>
<p>Some consumer routers also use the Linux kernel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> which is also the case for a wide variety of other devices, such as servers, <a href="/wiki/Smart_TV" title="Smart TV">smart TVs</a>, and other non-Android embedded systems. The kernel's use in traditional (non-server) desktop <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a> varieties is dominant, but the <a href="/wiki/Usage_share_of_operating_systems" title="Usage share of operating systems">usage share of Linux itself is low in comparison to other operating systems</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_aspects">Legal aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Legal aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Licensing_terms">Licensing terms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Licensing terms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Initially, Torvalds released Linux under a license which forbade any commercial use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hiroo_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-hiroo-35">[35]</a></sup> This was changed in version 0.12 to the <a href="/wiki/GNU_General_Public_License" title="GNU General Public License">GNU General Public License</a> (GPL).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Relnotes-0.12_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Relnotes-0.12-19">[19]</a></sup> This license allows distribution and sale of possibly modified and unmodified versions of Linux but requires that all those copies be released under the same license and be accompanied by the complete corresponding source code.</p>
<p>Torvalds has described licensing Linux under the GPL as the "best thing I ever did."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hiroo_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-hiroo-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="GPL_version_3">GPL version 3</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: GPL version 3">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Linux kernel is licensed explicitly only under version 2 of the GPL,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COPYING_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-COPYING-6">[6]</a></sup> without offering the licensee the option to choose "any later version", which is a common GPL extension. There was a debate over how easily it could be changed to use later GPL versions such as version 3 and whether this is even desirable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Torvalds himself specifically indicated upon the release of version 2.4.0 that his own code is only under version 2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> However, the terms of the GPL state that if no version is specified, then any version may be used,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (September 2016)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and <a href="/wiki/Alan_Cox" title="Alan Cox">Alan Cox</a> pointed out that very few other Linux contributors have specified a particular version of the GPL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>In September 2006, a survey of 29 key kernel programmers indicated that 28 preferred GPLv2 to the then-current GPLv3 draft. Torvalds commented, "I think a number of outsiders... believed that I personally was just the odd man out, because I've been so publicly not a huge fan of the GPLv3."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> This group of high-profile kernel developers, including <a href="/wiki/Linus_Torvalds" title="Linus Torvalds">Linus Torvalds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greg_Kroah-Hartman" title="Greg Kroah-Hartman">Greg Kroah-Hartman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Morton_(computer_programmer)" title="Andrew Morton (computer programmer)">Andrew Morton</a>, commented on mass media about their objections to the GPLv3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kerneldevelopers2006_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-kerneldevelopers2006-40">[40]</a></sup> They referred to clauses regarding <a href="/wiki/Digital_rights_management" title="Digital rights management">DRM</a>/<a href="/wiki/Tivoization" title="Tivoization">tivoization</a>, patents, "additional restrictions" and warned a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balkanisation" title="Balkanisation">Balkanisation</a> of the "Open Source Universe" by the GPLv3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kerneldevelopers2006_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-kerneldevelopers2006-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Linus Torvalds, who decided not to adopt the GPLv3 for the Linux kernel, reiterated his criticism even years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loadable_kernel_modules"><span id="DMA-BUF"></span>Loadable kernel modules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Loadable kernel modules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It is debated whether <a href="/wiki/Loadable_kernel_module" title="Loadable kernel module">loadable kernel modules</a> (LKMs) are to be considered <a href="/wiki/Derivative_work" title="Derivative work">derivative works</a> under copyright law, and thereby fall under the terms of the GPL.</p>
<p>Torvalds has stated his belief that LKMs using only a limited, "public" subset of the kernel interfaces can sometimes be non-derived works, thus allowing some binary-only drivers and other LKMs that are not licensed under the GPL.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A very good example for this is the usage of <i>dma_buf</i> by the proprietary <a href="/wiki/Nvidia" title="Nvidia">Nvidia</a> graphics drivers. dma_buf is a recent kernel feature (like the rest of the kernel, it is licensed under the GPL) that allows multiple GPUs to quickly copy data into each other's framebuffers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> One possible use case would be <a href="/wiki/Nvidia_Optimus" title="Nvidia Optimus">Nvidia Optimus</a> that pairs a fast GPU with an Intel integrated GPU, where the Nvidia GPU writes into the <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a> framebuffer when it is active. But, Nvidia cannot use this infrastructure because it uses a technical means to enforce the rule that it can only be used by LKMs that are also GPL. <a href="/wiki/Alan_Cox" title="Alan Cox">Alan Cox</a> replied on <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel_mailing_list" title="Linux kernel mailing list">LKML</a>, rejecting a request from one of their engineers to remove this technical enforcement from the API.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Not all Linux kernel contributors agree with this interpretation, however, and even Torvalds agrees that many LKMs are clearly derived works, and indeed he writes that "kernel modules ARE derivative 'by default'".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the other hand, Torvalds has also said that "one gray area in particular is something like a driver that was originally written for another operating system (i.e. clearly not a derived work of Linux in origin). [...] THAT is a gray area, and _that_ is the area where I personally believe that some modules may be considered to not be derived works simply because they weren't designed for Linux and don't depend on any special Linux behaviour."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Proprietary_software" title="Proprietary software">Proprietary</a> graphics drivers, in particular, are heavily discussed. Ultimately, it is likely that such questions can only be resolved by a court.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Firmware_binary_blobs">Firmware binary blobs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Firmware binary blobs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One point of licensing controversy is the use of <a href="/wiki/Firmware" title="Firmware">firmware</a> "<a href="/wiki/Binary_blob" title="Binary blob">binary blobs</a>" in Linux kernel to support several hardware devices. These files are under a variety of licenses, out of which many are restrictive and their exact underlying source code is usually unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-firmware_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-firmware-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2002, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Stallman" title="Richard Stallman">Richard Stallman</a> stated why, in his point of view, such blobs make the Linux kernel partially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-free_software" title="Non-free software">non-free software</a>, and that distributing Linux kernel "violates the GPL", which requires "complete corresponding source code" to be available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blobs_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blobs-7">[7]</a></sup> In 2008, <a href="/wiki/Free_Software_Foundation_Latin_America" title="Free Software Foundation Latin America">Free Software Foundation Latin America</a> started <a href="/wiki/Linux-libre" title="Linux-libre">Linux-libre</a> as a project that creates a completely free variant of the Linux kernel without proprietary objects; it is used by certain completely free Linux distributions, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Linux_distributions_endorsed_by_the_Free_Software_Foundation" title="List of Linux distributions endorsed by the Free Software Foundation">those endorsed by the Free Software Foundation</a>, while it can also be used on most distributions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 15 December 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Debian" title="Debian">Debian Project</a> announced that the next Debian stable version "6.0 Squeeze" would come with a kernel "stripped of all non-free firmware bits".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> This policy was continued to be applied in later stable Debian releases.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trademark">Trademark</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Trademark">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Linux#Copyright.2C_trademark.2C_and_naming" title="Linux">Linux: Copyright, trademark, and naming</a></div>
<p>Linux is a registered <a href="/wiki/Trademark" title="Trademark">trademark</a> of <a href="/wiki/Linus_Torvalds" title="Linus Torvalds">Linus Torvalds</a> in the United States and some other countries. This is the result of an incident in which William Della Croce, Jr., who was not involved in the Linux project, trademarked the name and subsequently demanded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royalties" title="Royalties">royalties</a> for its use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Several Linux backers retained legal counsel and filed suit against Della Croce. The issue was settled in August 1997 when the trademark was assigned to Linus Torvalds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="SCO_litigation">SCO litigation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: SCO litigation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SCO-Linux_controversies" title="SCO-Linux controversies">SCO-Linux controversies</a>.</div>
<p>In March 2003, the <a href="/wiki/SCO_Group" title="SCO Group">SCO Group</a> (SCO) filed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SCO_v._IBM" title="SCO v. IBM">lawsuit</a> against <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> claiming that IBM had violated copyrights that SCO claimed to hold over the <a href="/wiki/Unix" title="Unix">Unix</a> source code, by contributing portions of that code to Linux. Additionally, SCO sent letters to a number of companies warning that their use of Linux without a license from SCO may be a violation of copyright law, and claimed in the press that they would be suing individual Linux users. IBM then promised to defend its Linux customers on their behalf. This controversy has generated lawsuits by SCO against <a href="/wiki/Novell" title="Novell">Novell</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DaimlerChrysler" title="DaimlerChrysler">DaimlerChrysler</a> (partially dismissed in July 2004), and <a href="/wiki/AutoZone" title="AutoZone">AutoZone</a>, and retaliatory lawsuits by <a href="/wiki/Red_Hat" title="Red Hat">Red Hat</a> and others against SCO.</p>
<p>In early 2007, SCO filed the specific details of the purported copyright infringement. Despite previous claims that SCO was the rightful owner of 1 million lines of code, they specified 326 lines of code, most of which were uncopyrightable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> In August 2007, the court in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SCO_v._Novell" title="SCO v. Novell">the Novell case</a> ruled that SCO did not actually own the Unix copyrights to begin with,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> though the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenth_Circuit_Court_of_Appeals" title="Tenth Circuit Court of Appeals">Tenth Circuit Court of Appeals</a> ruled in August 2009 that the question of who owned the copyright properly remained for a jury to answer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The jury case was decided on 30 March 2010 in Novell's favour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Vmlinux" title="Vmlinux">vmlinux</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linux_kernel_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Linux_kernel_map.png/300px-Linux_kernel_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Linux_kernel_map.png/450px-Linux_kernel_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Linux_kernel_map.png/600px-Linux_kernel_map.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linux_kernel_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the Linux kernel</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linux_kernel_ubiquity.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1280" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Linux_kernel_ubiquity.svg/300px-Linux_kernel_ubiquity.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Linux_kernel_ubiquity.svg/450px-Linux_kernel_ubiquity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Linux_kernel_ubiquity.svg/600px-Linux_kernel_ubiquity.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linux_kernel_ubiquity.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Linux kernel supports various hardware architectures, providing a common platform for running software.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Linux kernel is a <a href="/wiki/Monolithic_kernel" title="Monolithic kernel">monolithic kernel</a>, supporting true <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preemptive_multitasking" title="Preemptive multitasking">preemptive multitasking</a> (both in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/User_mode" title="User mode">user mode</a> and, since the 2.6 series, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kernel_mode" title="Kernel mode">kernel mode</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lwn-22912_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-lwn-22912-57">[57]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Virtual_memory" title="Virtual memory">virtual memory</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Library_(computer_science)" title="Library (computer science)">shared libraries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demand_paging" title="Demand paging">demand loading</a>, shared <a href="/wiki/Copy-on-write" title="Copy-on-write">copy-on-write</a> executables (via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kernel_SamePage_Merging_(KSM)" title="Kernel SamePage Merging (KSM)">KSM</a>), <a href="/wiki/Memory_management" title="Memory management">memory management</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Internet_protocol_suite" title="Internet protocol suite">Internet protocol suite</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thread_(computer_science)" title="Thread (computer science)">threading</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Device_driver" title="Device driver">Device drivers</a> and kernel extensions run in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kernel_space" title="Kernel space">kernel space</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ring_(computer_security)" title="Ring (computer security)">ring 0</a> in many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CPU" title="CPU">CPU</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CPU_architecture" title="CPU architecture">architectures</a>), with full access to the hardware, although some exceptions run in <a href="/wiki/User_space" title="User space">user space</a>, for example filesystems based on <a href="/wiki/Filesystem_in_Userspace" title="Filesystem in Userspace">FUSE</a>/CUSE, and parts of UIO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/X_Window_System" title="X Window System">graphics system</a> most people use with Linux does not run within the kernel. Unlike standard monolithic kernels, device drivers are easily configured as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Module_(Linux)" title="Module (Linux)">modules</a>, and loaded or unloaded while the system is running. Also, unlike standard monolithic kernels, device drivers can be pre-empted under certain conditions; this feature was added to handle <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hardware_interrupt" title="Hardware interrupt">hardware interrupts</a> correctly, and to better support <a href="/wiki/Symmetric_multiprocessing" title="Symmetric multiprocessing">symmetric multiprocessing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lwn-22912_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-lwn-22912-57">[57]</a></sup> By choice, the Linux kernel has no <a href="/wiki/Application_binary_interface" title="Application binary interface">binary kernel interface</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>The hardware is also incorporated into the file hierarchy. Device drivers interface to user applications via an entry in the <span style="font-family: monospace, monospace;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Devfs" title="Devfs">/dev</a></span> or <span style="font-family: monospace, monospace;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki//sys" title="/sys">/sys</a></span> directories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tldp_file_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-tldp_file-61">[61]</a></sup> Process information as well is mapped to the file system through the <span style="font-family: monospace, monospace;"><a href="/wiki/Procfs" title="Procfs">/proc</a></span> directory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tldp_file_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-tldp_file-61">[61]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Programming_language">Programming language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Programming language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Linux kernel is written in the version of the <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C programming language</a> supported by <a href="/wiki/GNU_Compiler_Collection" title="GNU Compiler Collection">GCC</a> (which has introduced a number of extensions and changes to standard C), together with a number of short sections of code written in the <a href="/wiki/Assembly_language" title="Assembly language">assembly language</a> (in GCC's "AT&amp;T-style" syntax) of the target architecture. Because of the extensions to C it supports, GCC was for a long time the only compiler capable of correctly building the Linux kernel.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compiler_compatibility"><span id="LLVMLINUX"></span>Compiler compatibility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Compiler compatibility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/GNU_Compiler_Collection" title="GNU Compiler Collection">GCC</a> is the default <a href="/wiki/Compiler" title="Compiler">compiler</a> for the Linux kernel source. In 2004, <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a> claimed to have modified the kernel so that <a href="/wiki/Intel_C%2B%2B_Compiler" title="Intel C++ Compiler">its C compiler</a> was also capable of compiling it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> There was another such reported success in 2009, with a modified 2.6.22 version of the kernel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since 2010, effort has been underway to build the Linux kernel with <a href="/wiki/Clang" title="Clang">Clang</a>, an alternative compiler for the C language;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> as of 12 April 2014, the official kernel could almost be compiled by Clang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The project dedicated to this effort is named <i>LLVMLinux</i> after the <a href="/wiki/LLVM" title="LLVM">LLVM</a> compiler infrastructure upon which Clang is built.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> LLVMLinux does not aim to fork either the Linux kernel or the LLVM, therefore it is a meta-project composed of patches that are eventually submitted to the upstream projects. By enabling the Linux kernel to be compiled by Clang that, among other advantages, is known for faster compilation compared with GCC, kernel developers may benefit from a faster workflow due to shorter compilation times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interfaces">Interfaces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Interfaces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
