</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linux_AMD_graphics_stack.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1280" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Linux_AMD_graphics_stack.svg/300px-Linux_AMD_graphics_stack.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Linux_AMD_graphics_stack.svg/450px-Linux_AMD_graphics_stack.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Linux_AMD_graphics_stack.svg/600px-Linux_AMD_graphics_stack.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linux_AMD_graphics_stack.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
At XDC2014, Alex Deucher from AMD announced the unified kernel-mode driver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The proprietary Linux graphic driver, <span style="font-family: monospace, monospace;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AMD_Catalyst" title="AMD Catalyst">libGL-fglrx-glx</a></span>, will share the same <a href="/wiki/Direct_Rendering_Manager" title="Direct Rendering Manager">DRM</a> infrastructure with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesa_3D" title="Mesa 3D">Mesa 3D</a>. As there is no stable in-kernel <a href="/wiki/Application_binary_interface" title="Application binary interface">ABI</a>, AMD had to constantly adapt the former <a href="/wiki/Binary_blob" title="Binary blob">binary blob</a> used by Catalyst.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Conformance to standards is a general policy for the Linux kernel's internals. Another rule is that a kernel component is not accepted into the Linux kernel mainline if there is only proprietary user-space software using that component.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kernel-to-userspace_API">Kernel-to-userspace API</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Kernel-to-userspace API">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/POSIX" title="POSIX">POSIX</a> and <a href="/wiki/Single_UNIX_Specification" title="Single UNIX Specification">Single UNIX Specification</a></div>
<p>Source code portability ensures that a C program written by conforming to a standard can be successfully compiled and run on any system that also conforms to the same standard. The relevant standards, aiming to achieve source code portability of programs, that the development of the Linux kernel, the <a href="/wiki/GNU_C_Library" title="GNU C Library">GNU C Library</a>, and associated utilities tries to adhere to, are <a href="/wiki/POSIX" title="POSIX">POSIX</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Single_UNIX_Specification" title="Single UNIX Specification">Single UNIX Specification</a>. However, as of February 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, no Linux distributions are branded as "UNIX" by The Open Group, mainly because of the costs of the conformance testing.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linux_kernel_API" title="Linux kernel API">Linux kernel API</a> of the Linux kernel, representing the kernel's system call interface, is composed of the available <a href="/wiki/System_call" title="System call">system calls</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kernel-to-userspace_ABI">Kernel-to-userspace ABI</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Kernel-to-userspace ABI">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Linux_Standard_Base" title="Linux Standard Base">Linux Standard Base</a></div>
<p>Binary portability shall guarantee that any program once compiled for a given hardware platform, can be run in its compiled form on any other hardware platform that conforms to the standard. Binary portability is an essential requirement for the commercial viability of <a href="/wiki/Independent_software_vendor" title="Independent software vendor">independent software vendor</a> (ISV) applications built for the operating systems based on the Linux kernel. Binary compatibility is much more demanding than source code portability; as of February 2014, the only standard concerning itself with binary compatibility is the <a href="/wiki/Linux_Standard_Base" title="Linux Standard Base">Linux Standard Base</a> (LSB).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In-kernel_API">In-kernel API</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: In-kernel API">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are a couple of kernel internal APIs utilized between the different subsystems and subsystems of subsystems. Some of them have been kept stable over several releases, other have not. There are no guarantees regarding the in-kernel APIs. Maintainers and contributors are free to augment or change them at any time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Examples of in-kernel APIs include software frameworks/APIs for the following classes of device drivers:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Video4Linux" title="Video4Linux">Video4Linux</a> –  for video capture hardware</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Advanced_Linux_Sound_Architecture" title="Advanced Linux Sound Architecture">Advanced Linux Sound Architecture</a> (ALSA) –  for sound cards</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/New_API" title="New API">New API</a> –  for <a href="/wiki/Network_interface_controller" title="Network interface controller">network interface controllers</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Direct_Rendering_Manager" title="Direct Rendering Manager">Direct Rendering Manager</a> –  for graphics accelerators</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KMS_driver" title="KMS driver">KMS driver</a> –  for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Display_controller" title="Display controller">display controllers</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mac80211" title="Mac80211">mac80211</a> –  for wireless network interface controllers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></li>
<li>WEXT –  for wireless network interface controllers (obsolete).</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In-kernel_ABI">In-kernel ABI</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: In-kernel ABI">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some organizations have strongly supported defining and maintaining of a stable in-kernel <a href="/wiki/Application_binary_interface" title="Application binary interface">ABI</a> over several releases.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> For example, it would benefit hardware manufactures which release proprietary kernel modules and distribute binary-only software (e.g. device drivers).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> However, the Linux kernel developers choose not to maintain a stable in-kernel ABI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> This allows Linux kernel development to happen much more quickly.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technical_features">Technical features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Technical features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Preemption"><span id="PREEMPTION"></span>Preemption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Preemption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Kernel_preemption" title="Kernel preemption">Kernel preemption</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IO_stack_of_the_Linux_kernel.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1380" data-file-width="1052" height="577" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/IO_stack_of_the_Linux_kernel.svg/440px-IO_stack_of_the_Linux_kernel.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/IO_stack_of_the_Linux_kernel.svg/660px-IO_stack_of_the_Linux_kernel.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/IO_stack_of_the_Linux_kernel.svg/880px-IO_stack_of_the_Linux_kernel.svg.png 2x" width="440"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IO_stack_of_the_Linux_kernel.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The position of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I/O_scheduler" title="I/O scheduler">I/O schedulers</a> within various layers of the Linux kernel's storage stack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Linux kernel provides <a href="/wiki/Preemption_(computing)" title="Preemption (computing)">preemptive scheduling</a> under certain conditions. Until kernel version 2.4, only user processes were preemptive, i.e., in addition to time quantum expiration, an execution of current process in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/User_mode" title="User mode">user mode</a> would be interrupted if higher dynamic priority processes entered <code>TASK_RUNNING</code> state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Toward 2.6 series of the Linux kernel, an ability to interrupt a task executing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kernel_mode" title="Kernel mode">kernel code</a> was added, although with that not all sections of the kernel code can be preempted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Linux kernel contains different scheduler classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moshe_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-moshe-77">[77]</a></sup> By default the kernel uses a scheduler mechanism called the <a href="/wiki/Completely_Fair_Scheduler" title="Completely Fair Scheduler">Completely Fair Scheduler</a> introduced in the 2.6.23 version of the kernel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Internally this default-scheduler class is also known as <code>SCHED_OTHER</code>, but the kernel also contains two POSIX-compliant<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-posix1b_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-posix1b-79">[79]</a></sup> real-time scheduling classes named <code>SCHED_FIFO</code> (realtime <a href="/wiki/FIFO_(computing_and_electronics)" title="FIFO (computing and electronics)">first-in-first-out</a>) and <code>SCHED_RR</code> (realtime <a href="/wiki/Round-robin_scheduling" title="Round-robin scheduling">round-robin</a>), both of which take precedence over the default class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moshe_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-moshe-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Through the use of the real-time Linux kernel patch <code>PREEMPT_RT</code>, support for full preemption of critical sections, interrupt handlers, and "interrupt disable" code sequences can be supported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Partial mainline integration of the real-time Linux kernel patch already brought some functionality to the kernel mainline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Preemption improves <a href="/wiki/Latency_(engineering)" title="Latency (engineering)">latency</a>, increases responsiveness, and makes Linux more suitable for desktop and <a href="/wiki/Real-time_computing" title="Real-time computing">real-time</a> applications. Older versions of the kernel had a so-called <a href="/wiki/Giant_lock" title="Giant lock">big kernel lock</a> for synchronization across the entire kernel, which was finally removed by Arnd Bergmann in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Additional scheduling policy known as <code><a href="/wiki/SCHED_DEADLINE" title="SCHED DEADLINE">SCHED_DEADLINE</a></code>, implementing the <a href="/wiki/Earliest_deadline_first_scheduling" title="Earliest deadline first scheduling">earliest deadline first algorithm</a> (EDF), was added in kernel version 3.14, released on 30 March 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Portability">Portability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Portability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Linux_supported_architectures" title="List of Linux supported architectures">List of Linux supported architectures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Linux-powered_device" title="Linux-powered device">Linux-powered device</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ipod_linux_booting_kernel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="798" data-file-width="700" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Ipod_linux_booting_kernel.jpg/220px-Ipod_linux_booting_kernel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Ipod_linux_booting_kernel.jpg/330px-Ipod_linux_booting_kernel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Ipod_linux_booting_kernel.jpg/440px-Ipod_linux_booting_kernel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ipod_linux_booting_kernel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An <a href="/wiki/IPod" title="IPod">iPod</a> booting <a href="/wiki/IPodLinux" title="IPodLinux">iPodLinux</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While not originally designed to be <a href="/wiki/Porting" title="Porting">portable</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Torvlads25Aug91_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Torvlads25Aug91-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-opensources_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-opensources-85">[85]</a></sup> Linux is now one of the most widely ported operating system kernels, running on a diverse range of systems from the <a href="/wiki/ARM_architecture" title="ARM architecture">ARM architecture</a> to IBM <a href="/wiki/Z/Architecture" title="Z/Architecture">Z/Architecture</a> <a href="/wiki/Mainframe_computer" title="Mainframe computer">mainframe computers</a>. The first port beyond Linux's original 386 architecture was to the <a href="/wiki/Motorola_68000" title="Motorola 68000">Motorola 68000</a> platform by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commodore_Amiga" title="Commodore Amiga">Amiga</a> users, but to do this port they replaced major parts of the kernel, leading Torvalds to call it a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fork_(software)" title="Fork (software)">fork</a> and a "Linux-like operating system".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-opensources_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-opensources-85">[85]</a></sup> With this experience in mind, Torvalds led a major restructure of the kernel code to facilitate the next port effort, to the <a href="/wiki/Digital_Equipment_Corporation" title="Digital Equipment Corporation">DEC</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha_AXP" title="Alpha AXP">Alpha AXP</a> platform, and support both 386 and Alpha in a single source tree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-opensources_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-opensources-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Linux runs as the main operating system on IBM's <a href="/wiki/Blue_Gene" title="Blue Gene">Blue Gene</a> and other fastest <a href="/wiki/Supercomputer" title="Supercomputer">supercomputers</a>. As of June 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 99+% of the world's <a href="/wiki/TOP500" title="TOP500">500 fastest supercomputers</a> run some variant of Linux,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-top500stats_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-top500stats-86">[86]</a></sup> including the top 280.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-top500-list_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-top500-list-87">[87]</a></sup> Linux has also been ported to various handheld devices such as <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple's</a> <a href="/wiki/IPod" title="IPod">iPod</a> and <a href="/wiki/IPhone" title="IPhone">iPhone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Some operating systems developed for mobile phones use modified versions of the Linux kernel, including Google <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a>, <a href="/wiki/Firefox_OS" title="Firefox OS">Firefox OS</a>, HP <a href="/wiki/WebOS" title="WebOS">webOS</a>, and Nokia <a href="/wiki/Maemo" title="Maemo">Maemo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kernel_panic_and_oopses">Kernel panic and oopses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Kernel panic and oopses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kernel-panic.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1706" data-file-width="2560" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Kernel-panic.jpg/220px-Kernel-panic.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Kernel-panic.jpg/330px-Kernel-panic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Kernel-panic.jpg/440px-Kernel-panic.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kernel-panic.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An example of Linux kernel panic</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Kernel_panic" title="Kernel panic">Kernel panic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel_oops" title="Linux kernel oops">Linux kernel oops</a></div>
<p>In Linux, a "<a href="/wiki/Kernel_panic" title="Kernel panic">panic</a>" is an unrecoverable system error detected by the kernel, as opposed to similar errors detected by <a href="/wiki/User_space" title="User space">user space</a> code. It is possible for kernel code to indicate such a condition by calling the <code>panic</code> function located in the header file <tt>sys/system.h</tt>. However, most panics are the result of unhandled processor <a href="/wiki/Exception_handling" title="Exception handling">exceptions</a> in kernel code, such as references to invalid memory addresses. These are typically indicative of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computer_bug" title="Computer bug">bug</a> somewhere in the call chain leading to the panic. They can also indicate a failure of hardware, such as a failed RAM cell or errors in arithmetic functions in the processor caused by a <a href="/wiki/Pentium_FDIV_bug" title="Pentium FDIV bug">processor bug</a>, overheating/damaged processor, or a <a href="/wiki/Soft_error" title="Soft error">soft error</a>.</p>
<p>A report of a non-fatal bug in the kernel is called an "<a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel_oops" title="Linux kernel oops">oops</a>"; such deviations from correct behavior of the Linux kernel may allow continued operation with compromised reliability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> These crash reports are automatically collected and can be sent upstream by various software, such as kerneloops,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> ABRT (<a href="/wiki/Fedora_(operating_system)" title="Fedora (operating system)">Fedora</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> and apport (<a href="/wiki/Ubuntu_(operating_system)" title="Ubuntu (operating system)">Ubuntu</a>). KernelOops.org collected these reports and published statistics on their website.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>The kernel panic message might not be printed visibly in some conditions, such as when using a graphical desktop. To debug such conditions, other methods such as attaching a serial port console can be used.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Live_patching">Live patching</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Live patching">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Rebootless updates can even be applied to the kernel by using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Live_patching" title="Live patching">live patching</a> technologies such as <a href="/wiki/Ksplice" title="Ksplice">Ksplice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kpatch" title="Kpatch">kpatch</a> and <a href="/wiki/KGraft" title="KGraft">kGraft</a>. Minimalistic foundations for live kernel patching were merged into the Linux kernel mainline in kernel version 4.0, which was released on 12 April 2015. Those foundations, known as <i>livepatch</i> and based primarily on the kernel's <a href="/wiki/Ftrace" title="Ftrace">ftrace</a> functionality, form a common core capable of supporting hot patching by both kGraft and kpatch, by providing an <a href="/wiki/Application_programming_interface" title="Application programming interface">application programming interface</a> (API) for kernel modules that contain hot patches and an <a href="/wiki/Application_binary_interface" title="Application binary interface">application binary interface</a> (ABI) for the userspace management utilities. However, the common core included into Linux kernel 4.0 supports only the <a href="/wiki/X86" title="X86">x86</a> architecture and does not provide any mechanisms for ensuring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Function_(programming)" title="Function (programming)">function</a>-level consistency while the hot patches are applied. As of April 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there is ongoing work on porting kpatch and kGraft to the common live patching core provided by the Linux kernel mainline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Security">Security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Computer_security" title="Computer security">Computer security</a> is a much-publicized topic in relation to the Linux kernel because a large portion of the kernel bugs present potential security flaws. For example, they may allow for <a href="/wiki/Privilege_escalation" title="Privilege escalation">privilege escalation</a> or create <a href="/wiki/Denial-of-service_attack" title="Denial-of-service attack">denial-of-service attack</a> vectors. Over the years, numerous such flaws were found and fixed in the Linux kernel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> New security features are frequently implemented to improve the Linux kernel's security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics have accused kernel developers of covering up security flaws or at least not announcing them; in 2008, Linus Torvalds responded to this with the following:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I personally consider security bugs to be just "normal bugs". I don't cover them up, but I also don't have any reason what-so-ever to think it's a good idea to track them and announce them as something special...one reason I refuse to bother with the whole security circus is that I think it glorifies—and thus encourages—the wrong behavior. It makes "heroes" out of security people, as if the people who don't just fix normal bugs aren't as important. In fact, all the boring normal bugs are <i>way</i> more important, just because there's a lot more of them. I don't think some spectacular security hole should be glorified or cared about as being any more "special" than a random spectacular crash due to bad locking.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Linux distributions typically release security updates to fix vulnerabilities in the Linux kernel. Many offer <a href="/wiki/Long-term_support" title="Long-term support">long-term support</a> releases that receive security updates for a certain Linux kernel version for an extended period of time.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feature_history">Feature history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Feature history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Version 1.0 of the Linux kernel was released on 14 March 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> This release of the Linux kernel only supported <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uniprocessor" title="Uniprocessor">single-processor</a> <a href="/wiki/IA-32" title="IA-32">i386</a>-based computer systems. Portability became a concern, and so version 1.2 (released 7 March 1995)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> gained support for computer systems using processors based on the <a href="/wiki/DEC_Alpha" title="DEC Alpha">Alpha</a>, <a href="/wiki/SPARC" title="SPARC">SPARC</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIPS_architecture" title="MIPS architecture">MIPS</a> architectures.</p>
<p>Version 2.0 was released on 9 June 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2.0.0_release_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-2.0.0_release-106">[106]</a></sup> There were 41 releases in the series. The major feature of 2.0 was <a href="/wiki/Symmetric_multiprocessing" title="Symmetric multiprocessing">SMP</a> support (that is, support for multiple processors in a single system) and support for more types of processors.</p>
<p>Version 2.2, released on 20 January 1999,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2.2.0_release_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-2.2.0_release-107">[107]</a></sup> removed the global <a href="/wiki/Spinlock" title="Spinlock">spinlock</a> and provided improved SMP support, added support for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M68k" title="M68k">m68k</a> and <a href="/wiki/PowerPC" title="PowerPC">PowerPC</a> architectures, and added new file systems (including read-only support for <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>'s <a href="/wiki/NTFS" title="NTFS">NTFS</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Version 2.4.0, released on 4 January 2001,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2.4.0_release_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-2.4.0_release-109">[109]</a></sup> contained support for <a href="/wiki/Industry_Standard_Architecture" title="Industry Standard Architecture">ISA</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plug_and_Play" title="Plug and Play">Plug and Play</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_Serial_Bus" title="Universal Serial Bus">USB</a>, and <a href="/wiki/PC_Card" title="PC Card">PC Cards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WWOL24_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-WWOL24-110">[110]</a></sup> It also included support for the <a href="/wiki/PA-RISC" title="PA-RISC">PA-RISC</a> processor from <a href="/wiki/Hewlett-Packard" title="Hewlett-Packard">Hewlett-Packard</a>. Development for 2.4.<i>x</i> changed a bit in that more features were made available throughout the duration of the series, including: support for <a href="/wiki/Bluetooth" title="Bluetooth">Bluetooth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Logical_Volume_Manager_(Linux)" title="Logical Volume Manager (Linux)">Logical Volume Manager</a> (LVM) version 1, <a href="/wiki/RAID" title="RAID">RAID</a> support, <a href="/wiki/InterMezzo_(file_system)" title="InterMezzo (file system)">InterMezzo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ext3" title="Ext3">ext3</a> file systems.</p>
<p>Version 2.6.0 was released on 17 December 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2.6.0_release_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-2.6.0_release-111">[111]</a></sup> The development for 2.6.<i>x</i> changed further towards including new features throughout the duration of the series. Among the changes that have been made in the 2.6 series are: integration of <a href="/wiki/%CE%9CClinux" title="ΜClinux">µClinux</a> into the mainline kernel sources, <a href="/wiki/Physical_Address_Extension" title="Physical Address Extension">PAE</a> support, support for several new lines of <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">CPUs</a>, integration of Advanced Linux Sound Architecture (ALSA) into the mainline kernel sources, support for up to 2<sup>32</sup> users (up from 2<sup>16</sup>), support for up to 2<sup>29</sup> process IDs (64-bit only, 32-bit arches still limited to 2<sup>15</sup>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> substantially increased the number of device types and the number of devices of each type, improved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/64-bit" title="64-bit">64-bit</a> support, support for <a href="/wiki/File_system" title="File system">file systems</a> which support file sizes of up to 16 <a href="/wiki/Terabyte" title="Terabyte">terabytes</a>, in-kernel <a href="/wiki/Preemption_(computing)" title="Preemption (computing)">preemption</a>, support for the <a href="/wiki/Native_POSIX_Thread_Library" title="Native POSIX Thread Library">Native POSIX Thread Library</a> (NPTL), <a href="/wiki/User-mode_Linux" title="User-mode Linux">User-mode Linux</a> integration into the mainline kernel sources, <a href="/wiki/Security-Enhanced_Linux" title="Security-Enhanced Linux">SELinux</a> integration into the mainline kernel sources, <a href="/wiki/InfiniBand" title="InfiniBand">InfiniBand</a> support, and considerably more. Also notable are the addition of several file systems throughout the 2.6.<i>x</i> releases: <a href="/wiki/Filesystem_in_Userspace" title="Filesystem in Userspace">FUSE</a>, <a href="/wiki/JFS_(file_system)" title="JFS (file system)">JFS</a>, <a href="/wiki/XFS" title="XFS">XFS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ext4" title="Ext4">ext4</a> and more. Details on the history of the 2.6 kernel series can be found in the ChangeLog files on the 2.6 kernel series source code release area of <a href="/wiki/Kernel.org" title="Kernel.org">kernel.org</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>Version 3.0 was released on 22 July 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3.0_release_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-3.0_release-22">[22]</a></sup> On 30 May 2011, Torvalds announced that the big change was "NOTHING. Absolutely nothing." and asked "...let's make sure we really make the next release not just an all new shiny number, but a good kernel too."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> After the expected 6–7 weeks of the development process, it would be released near the 20th anniversary of Linux.</p>
<p>In December 2012, Torvalds decided to reduce kernel complexity by removing support for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I386" title="I386">i386</a> processors, making the 3.7 kernel series the last one still supporting the original processor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The same series unified support for the <a href="/wiki/ARM_architecture" title="ARM architecture">ARM</a> processor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>Version 3.11, released on 2 September 2013,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3.11.0_release_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-3.11.0_release-118">[118]</a></sup> adds many new features such as new <code>O_TMPFILE</code> flag for <span class="plainlinksneverexpand"><code><a class="external text" href="http://www.kernel.org/doc/man-pages/online/pages/man2/open.2.html" rel="nofollow">open(2)</a></code></span> to reduce temporary file vulnerabilities, experimental AMD <a href="/wiki/Radeon" title="Radeon">Radeon</a> dynamic power management, low-latency network polling, and <a href="/wiki/Zswap" title="Zswap">zswap</a> (compressed swap cache).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>There were no meaningful technical reasons for the numbering change from 2.6.39 to 3.0, or from 3.19 to 4.0. The major version number was increased just to avoid large minor numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3.0_release_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-3.0_release-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4.0_release_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-4.0_release-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="mw-graph" style="min-width:800px;min-height:400px"><img class="mw-graph-img" src="/api/rest_v1/page/graph/png/Linux_kernel/0/e2b0fac8e29ba3ebdb4116330cf5acea76ef73d4.png"/></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Developer_community">Developer community</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Developer community">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of 2007, the development of the kernel had shifted from the top 20 most active developers writing 80% of the code to the top 30 writing 30% of the code, with top developers spending more time reviewing changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-121">[121]</a></sup> Developers can also be categorized by affiliation; in 2007, the top category was unknown while the top corporation was Red Hat with 12% of contributions, and known amateurs at 3.9%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-121">[121]</a></sup> The kernel changes made in year 2007 have been submitted by over 1900 developers, which may be a significant underestimate because developers working in teams usually count as one.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It is generally assumed that the community of Linux kernel developers comprises 5000 or 6000 members.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Codebase">Codebase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Codebase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the 3.10 release of the Linux kernel had 15,803,499 lines of code. As of 2007, roughly 5% of the code is part of the "core" while 52% is drivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Instead of a roadmap, there are technical guidelines. Instead of a central resource allocation, there are persons and companies who all have a stake in the further development of the Linux kernel, quite independently from one another: People like Linus Torvalds and I don’t plan the kernel evolution. We don’t sit there and think up the roadmap for the next two years, then assign resources to the various new features. That's because we don’t have any resources. The resources are all owned by the various corporations who use and contribute to Linux, as well as by the various independent contributors out there. It's those people who own the resources who decide...</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Morton_(computer_programmer)" title="Andrew Morton (computer programmer)">Andrew Morton</a>, 2005</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Linux is evolution, not intelligent design!</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Linus_Torvalds" title="Linus Torvalds">Linus Torvalds</a>, 2005<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>By this statement it is meant that <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> often does odd (and "sub-optimal") things exactly because it does incremental changes which <i>do not break</i> at any point. As a result, any released version of the Linux kernel is fully usable, even if, for example, device drivers do not support all features of the hardware they are written for.</p>
<p>The conceptual architecture of the Linux kernel has proved its success, while essential factors for this success were the provision for the organization of developers, and the provision for system extensibility. The Linux kernel's architecture was required to support many independent volunteer developers, which suggested that the system portions that require the most development—<wbr></wbr>​​hardware device drivers, file systems and network protocols—<wbr></wbr>​​be implemented in an extensible fashion. The Linux kernel's architecture chose to make these systems extensible using a data abstraction technique –  each hardware device driver is implemented as a separate module that supports a common interface. In this way, a single developer can add a new device driver, with minimal interaction required with other developers of the Linux kernel.</p>
<p>Another important extension to the Linux kernel is the addition of more supported hardware platforms. The architecture of the system supports this extensibility by separating all hardware-specific code into distinct modules within each subsystem. In this way, a small group of developers can implement a port of the Linux kernel to a new hardware architecture by re-implementing only the machine-specific portions of the kernel.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Estimated_cost_to_redevelop">Estimated cost to redevelop</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Estimated cost to redevelop">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The cost to redevelop the Linux kernel version 2.6.0 in a traditional proprietary development setting has been estimated to be US$612 million (€467M, £394M) in 2004 prices using the <a href="/wiki/COCOMO" title="COCOMO">COCOMO</a> man-month estimation model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> In 2006, a study funded by the European Union put the redevelopment cost of kernel version 2.6.8 higher, at €882M ($1.14bn, £744M).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>This topic was revisited in October 2008 by Amanda McPherson, Brian Proffitt and Ron Hale-Evans. Using David A. Wheeler's methodology, they estimated redevelopment of the 2.6.25 kernel now costs $1.3bn (part of a total $10.8bn to redevelop Fedora 9).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Again, Garcia-Garcia and Alonso de Magdaleno from University of Oviedo (Spain) estimate that the value annually added to kernel was about €100M between 2005 and 2007 and €225M in 2008, it would cost also more than €1bn (about $1.4bn as of February 2010) to develop in the European Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 7 March 2011<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, using then-current LOC (lines of code) of a 2.6.x Linux kernel and wage numbers with David A. Wheeler's calculations it would cost approximately $3bn (about €2.2bn) to redevelop the Linux kernel as it keeps getting bigger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_model"><span id="MAINLINE"></span><span id="VANILLA"></span>Development model</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Development model">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(March 2011)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The current development model of the Linux kernel is such that Linus Torvalds makes the releases of new versions, also called the "<a href="/wiki/Vanilla_software" title="Vanilla software">vanilla</a>" or "mainline" kernels, meaning that they contain the main, generic branch of development. This branch is officially released as a new version approximately every ten weeks, after Torvalds does an initial round of integrating major changes made by all other programmers, and several rounds of bug-fix pre-releases.</p>
<p>As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, in the current development scheme, the main branch of development is not a traditional "stable" branch; instead, it incorporates all kinds of changes, including both the latest features, and security and bug fixes. For users who do not want to risk updating to new versions containing code that may not be well tested, a separate set of "stable" branches exist, one for each released version, which are meant for people who just want the security and bug fixes, but not a whole new version. These branches are maintained by the <i>stable team</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greg_Kroah-Hartman" title="Greg Kroah-Hartman">Greg Kroah-Hartman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chris_Wright_(programmer)" title="Chris Wright (programmer)">Chris Wright</a>, and others).</p>
<p>The development model for the 2.6 kernel series was significantly different compared to the 2.5 series. Before the 2.6 series, there was a stable branch (2.4) where only relatively minor and safe changes were merged, and an unstable branch (2.5), where bigger changes and cleanups were allowed. Both of these branches had been maintained by the same set of people, led by Torvalds. This meant that users would always have a well-tested 2.4 version with the latest security and bug fixes to use, though they would have to wait for the features which went into the 2.5 branch. The downside of this was that the "stable" kernel ended up so far behind that it no longer supported recent hardware and lacked needed features. In the late 2.5 kernel series, some maintainers elected to try backporting of their changes to the stable kernel series, which resulted in bugs being introduced into the 2.4 kernel series. The 2.5 branch was then eventually declared stable and renamed to 2.6. But instead of opening an unstable 2.7 branch, the kernel developers decided to continue putting major changes into the 2.6 branch, which would then be released at a pace faster than 2.4.x but slower than 2.5.x. This had the desirable effect of making new features more quickly available and getting more testing of the new code, which was added in smaller batches and easier to test.</p>
<p>As a response to the lack of a <i>stable</i> kernel tree where people could coordinate the collection of bug fixes as such, in December 2005 <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Adrian_Bunk&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Adrian Bunk (page does not exist)">Adrian Bunk</a> announced that he would keep releasing 2.6.16.y kernels when the stable team moved on to 2.6.17.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2.6.16-longterm_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-2.6.16-longterm-130">[130]</a></sup> He also included some driver updates, making the maintenance of the 2.6.16 series very similar to the old rules for maintenance of a stable series such as 2.4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Since then, the "stable team" had been formed, and it would keep updating kernel versions with bug fixes. In October 2008 Adrian Bunk announced that he will maintain 2.6.27 for a few years as a replacement of 2.6.16.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> The stable team picked up on the idea and as of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> they continue to maintain that version and release bug fixes for it, in addition to others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the change of the development model with 2.6.x, developers continued to want what one might call an <i>unstable</i> kernel tree, one that changes as rapidly as new patches come in. <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Morton_(computer_programmer)" title="Andrew Morton (computer programmer)">Andrew Morton</a> decided to repurpose his <a href="/wiki/Mm_tree" title="Mm tree">-mm tree</a> from memory management to serve as the destination for all new and experimental code. In September 2007, Morton decided to stop maintaining this tree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> In February 2008, Stephen Rothwell created the <i>linux-next</i> tree to serve as a place where patches aimed to be merged during the next development cycle are gathered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Several subsystem maintainers also adopted the suffix <i>-next</i> for trees containing code which is meant to be submitted for inclusion in the next release cycle.</p>
<p>As of January 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the in-development version of the Linux kernel is held in an unstable branch named <i>linux-next</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_with_Linux_distributions">Relation with Linux distributions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Relation with Linux distributions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Most Linux users run a kernel supplied by their <a href="/wiki/Linux_distribution" title="Linux distribution">Linux distribution</a>. Some distributions ship the "vanilla" or "stable" kernels. However, several Linux distribution vendors (such as <a href="/wiki/Red_Hat" title="Red Hat">Red Hat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Debian" title="Debian">Debian</a>) maintain another set of Linux kernel branches which are integrated into their products. These are usually updated at a slower pace compared to the "vanilla" branch, and they usually include all fixes from the relevant "stable" branch, but at the same time they can also add support for drivers or features which had not been released in the "vanilla" version the distribution vendor started basing their branch from.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maintenance">Maintenance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Maintenance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While Linus Torvalds supervises code changes and releases to the latest kernel versions, he has delegated the maintenance of older versions to other programmers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MAINTAINERS_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-MAINTAINERS-138">[138]</a></sup> Major releases as old as 2.0 (officially made obsolete with the kernel 2.2.0 release in January 1999) are maintained as needed, although at a very slow pace.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Releases_before_2.6.0"><span id="PRE-2.6.0"></span>Releases before 2.6.0</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Releases before 2.6.0">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2.6.x.y_releases"><span id="2.6.X.Y"></span>2.6.x.y releases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: 2.6.x.y releases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Versions 2.6.16 and 2.6.27 of the Linux kernel were unofficially supported in a <a href="/wiki/Long-term_support" title="Long-term support">long-term support</a> (LTS) fashion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> before a 2011 <a href="/wiki/Working_group" title="Working group">working group</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Linux_Foundation" title="Linux Foundation">Linux Foundation</a> started a formal long-term support initiative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revision_control">Revision control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Revision control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Linux kernel source code used to be maintained without the help of an automated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Source_code_management" title="Source code management">source code management</a> system, mostly because of Linus Torvalds' dislike of centralized SCM systems.</p>
<p>In 2002, Linux kernel development switched to <a href="/wiki/BitKeeper" title="BitKeeper">BitKeeper</a>, an SCM system which satisfied Torvalds' technical requirements. BitKeeper was made available to Linus and several others free of charge, but was not <a href="/wiki/Free_software" title="Free software">free software</a>, which was a source of controversy. The system did provide some interoperability with free SCM systems such as <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_Versions_System" title="Concurrent Versions System">CVS</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subversion_(software)" title="Subversion (software)">Subversion</a>.</p>
<p>In April 2005, however, efforts to reverse-engineer the BitKeeper system by <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Tridgell" title="Andrew Tridgell">Andrew Tridgell</a> led BitMover, the company which maintained BitKeeper, to stop supporting the Linux development community. In response, Torvalds and others wrote a new source code control system for the purpose, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Git_(software)" title="Git (software)">Git</a>. The new system was written within weeks, and in two months the first official kernel release was made using Git.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[304]</a></sup> Git soon developed into a separate project in its own right and gained widespread adoption.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Version_numbering">Version numbering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Version numbering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Linux kernel has had three different numbering schemes.</p>
<p>The first scheme was used in the run-up to "1.0". The first version of the kernel was 0.01. This was followed by 0.02, 0.03, 0.10, 0.11, 0.12 (the first GPL version), 0.95, 0.96, 0.97, 0.98, 0.99 and then 1.0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[305]</a></sup> From 0.95 on there were many patch releases between versions.</p>
<p>After the 1.0 release and prior to version 2.6, the number was composed as "a.b.c", where the number "a" denoted the kernel version, the number "b" denoted the major revision of the kernel, and the number "c" indicated the minor revision of the kernel. The kernel version was changed only when major changes in the code and the concept of the kernel occurred, twice in the history of the kernel: in 1994 (version 1.0) and in 1996 (version 2.0). Version 3.0 was released in 2011, but it was not a major change in kernel concept. The major revision was assigned according to the <a href="/wiki/Software_versioning#Odd-numbered_versions_for_development_releases" title="Software versioning">even–odd version numbering scheme</a>. The minor revision had been changed whenever security patches, bug fixes, new features or drivers were implemented in the kernel.</p>
<p>In 2004, after version 2.6.0 was released, the kernel developers held several discussions regarding the release and version scheme<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[306]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[307]</a></sup> and ultimately Linus Torvalds and others decided that a much shorter "time-based" release cycle would be beneficial. For about seven years, the first two numbers remained "2.6", and the third number was incremented with each new release, which rolled out after two to three months. A fourth number was sometimes added to account for bug and security fixes (only) to the kernel version. The even-odd system of alternation between stable and unstable was gone. Instead, development pre-releases are titled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Release_candidate" title="Release candidate">release candidates</a>, which is indicated by appending the suffix '-rc' to the kernel version, followed by an ordinal number.</p>
<p>The first use of the fourth number occurred when a grave error, which required immediate fixing, was encountered in 2.6.8's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Network_File_System_(protocol)" title="Network File System (protocol)">NFS</a> code. However, there were not enough other changes to legitimize the release of a new minor revision (which would have been 2.6.9). So, 2.6.8.1 was released, with the only change being the fix of that error. With 2.6.11, this was adopted as the new official versioning policy. Later it became customary to continuously back-port major bug-fixes and security patches to released kernels and indicate that by updating the fourth number.</p>
<p>On 29 May 2011, Linus Torvalds announced<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[308]</a></sup> that the kernel version would be bumped to 3.0 for the release following 2.6.39, due to the minor version number getting too large and to commemorate the 20th anniversary of Linux. It continued the time-based release practice introduced with 2.6.0, but using the second number; for example, 3.1 would follow 3.0 after a few months. An additional number (now the third number) would be added on when necessary to designate security and bug fixes, as for example with 3.0.18; the Linux community refers to this as "3.x.y.z" versioning. The major version number was also later raised to 4, for the release following version 3.19.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[309]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4.0-poll_279-1"><a href="#cite_note-4.0-poll-279">[b]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to Linus's "-rc" development releases, sometimes the version will have a suffix such as "tip", indicating another <a href="/wiki/Fork_(software_development)" title="Fork (software development)">development branch</a>, usually (but not always) the initials of a person who made it. For example, "ck" stands for <a href="/wiki/Con_Kolivas" title="Con Kolivas">Con Kolivas</a>, "ac" stands for <a href="/wiki/Alan_Cox" title="Alan Cox">Alan Cox</a>, etc. Sometimes, the letters are related to the primary development area of the branch the kernel is built from, for example, "wl" indicates a wireless networking test build. Also, distributors may have their own suffixes with different numbering systems and for back-ports to their "enterprise" (i.e. stable but older) distribution versions.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline">Timeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Timeline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="timeline-wrapper"><map name="timeline_5450bc20e0cd99964584ec9224cdeb75"></map><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/timeline/5450bc20e0cd99964584ec9224cdeb75.png" usemap="#timeline_5450bc20e0cd99964584ec9224cdeb75"/></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Variants">Variants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linux_kernel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Variants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are certain variants of the Linux kernel that provide additional functionality, but do not belong to the Linux kernel mainline. Such variants of the Linux kernel include <a href="/wiki/Linux-libre" title="Linux-libre">Linux-libre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Compute_Node_Linux" title="Compute Node Linux">Compute Node Linux</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cooperative_Linux" title="Cooperative Linux">Cooperative Linux</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grsecurity" title="Grsecurity">grsecurity</a>, <a href="/wiki/INK_(operating_system)" title="INK (operating system)">INK</a>, <a href="/wiki/L4Linux" title="L4Linux">L4Linux</a>, <a href="/wiki/MkLinux" title="MkLinux">MkLinux</a>, <a href="/wiki/RTLinux" title="RTLinux">RTLinux</a>, and <a href="/wiki/User-mode_Linux" title="User-mode Linux">User-mode Linux</a>. Some of these variants have been partially merged into the mainline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[310]</a></sup></p>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">11 March</span> 2015</span>. <q>The current version (Discussion Draft 2) of GPLv3 on first reading fails the necessity test of section 1 on the grounds that there's no substantial and identified problem with GPLv2 that it is trying to solve. <li id="cite_note-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-89">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a href="/wiki/Greg_Kroah-Hartman" title="Greg Kroah-Hartman">Kroah-Hartman, Greg</a> (2 February 2010). <a class="external text" href="http://www.kroah.com/log/linux/android-kernel-problems.html" rel="nofollow">"Android and the Linux kernel community"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">3 February</span> 2010</span>. <q><i>This means that any drivers written for Android hardware platforms, can not get merged into the main kernel tree because they have dependencies on code that only lives in Google's kernel tree, causing it to fail to build in the kernel.org tree. Because of this, Google has now prevented a large chunk of hardware drivers and platform code from ever getting merged into the main kernel tree. Effectively creating a kernel branch that a number of different vendors are now relying on.</i></q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALinux+kernel&amp;rft.aufirst=Greg&amp;rft.aulast=Kroah-Hartman&amp;rft.btitle=Android+and+the+Linux+kernel+community&amp;rft.date=2010-02-02&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.kroah.com%2Flog%2Flinux%2Fandroid-kernel-problems.html&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
